      <p>
       The
       <b>
        Lunar Atmosphere and Dust Environment Explorer
       </b>
       (
       <b>
        LADEE
       </b>
       ,
       <small>
        pronounced
       </small>
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
            l
           </span>
           <span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">
            æ
           </span>
           <span title="'d' in 'dye'">
            d
           </span>
           <span title="/./: syllable break">
            .
           </span>
           <span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">
            iː
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-overview_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa-overview-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) was a
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_exploration" title="Lunar exploration">
        lunar exploration
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Technology_demonstration" title="Technology demonstration">
        technology demonstration
       </a>
       mission. It was launched on a
       <a href="/wiki/Minotaur_V" title="Minotaur V">
        Minotaur V
       </a>
       rocket from the
       <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_Regional_Spaceport" title="Mid-Atlantic Regional Spaceport">
        Mid-Atlantic Regional Spaceport
       </a>
       on September 7, 2013.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gsfc-ladee_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-gsfc-ladee-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       During its seven-month mission, LADEE
       <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">
        orbited
       </a>
       around the Moon's equator, using its instruments to study the
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_the_Moon" title="Atmosphere of the Moon">
        lunar exosphere
       </a>
       and dust in the Moon's vicinity. Instruments included a dust detector, neutral
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometer" title="Mass spectrometer">
        mass spectrometer
       </a>
       , and ultraviolet-visible
       <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">
        spectrometer
       </a>
       , as well as a technology demonstration consisting of a
       <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">
        laser
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satellite_communications" title="Satellite communications">
        communications
       </a>
       terminal.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-science_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa-science-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The mission ended on April 18, 2014, when the spacecraft's controllers intentionally crashed LADEE into the
       <a href="/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon" title="Far side of the Moon">
        far side of the Moon
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20140418_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nyt20140418-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc20140418_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-abc20140418-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which, later, was determined to be near the eastern rim of
       <a href="/wiki/Sundman_(crater)" title="Sundman (crater)">
        Sundman V crater
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141028-NNJ_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141028-NNJ-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>
      <div class="toc" id="toc">

      <p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Planning_and_preparations">
        Planning and preparations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Planning and preparations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       LADEE was announced during the presentation of NASA's
       <abbr title="fiscal year 2009">
        FY09
       </abbr>
       budget in February 2008.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       It was initially planned to be launched with the
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity_Recovery_and_Interior_Laboratory" title="Gravity Recovery and Interior Laboratory">
        Gravity Recovery and Interior Laboratory
       </a>
       (GRAIL) satellites.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-2008_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa-2008-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Mechanical tests including
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_Field_Acoustic_Testing" title="Direct Field Acoustic Testing">
        acoustic
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Vibration#Vibration_testing" title="Vibration">
        vibration
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Shock_(mechanics)" title="Shock (mechanics)">
        shock
       </a>
       tests were completed prior to full-scale
       <a href="/wiki/Thermal_vacuum_chamber" title="Thermal vacuum chamber">
        thermal vacuum chamber
       </a>
       testing at NASA's
       <a href="/wiki/Ames_Research_Center" title="Ames Research Center">
        Ames Research Center
       </a>
       in April 2013.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20130430_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa20130430-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       During August 2013, LADEE underwent final balancing, fuelling and mounting on the launcher, and all pre-launch activities were complete by August 31, ready for the launch window which opened on September 6.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ready_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa-ready-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       NASA Ames was responsible for the day-to-day functions of LADEE while the
       <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">
        Goddard Space Flight Center
       </a>
       operated the sensor suite and technology demonstration payloads as well as managing launch operations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-role_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa-role-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The LADEE mission cost approximately $280 million, which included spacecraft development and science instruments, launch services, mission operations, science processing and relay support.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQ_1-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-FAQ-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_glow">
        Atmospheric glow
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Atmospheric glow">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_17_twilight_ray_sketch.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="620" data-file-width="450" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Apollo_17_twilight_ray_sketch.jpg/170px-Apollo_17_twilight_ray_sketch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Apollo_17_twilight_ray_sketch.jpg/255px-Apollo_17_twilight_ray_sketch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Apollo_17_twilight_ray_sketch.jpg/340px-Apollo_17_twilight_ray_sketch.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_17_twilight_ray_sketch.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         At sunrise and sunset various Apollo crews saw glows and rays.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storm_15-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-storm-15">
           [15]
          </a>
         </sup>
         This Apollo 17 sketch depicts the mysterious twilight rays.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Moon may have a tenuous atmosphere of moving particles constantly leaping up from and falling back to the Moon's surface, giving rise to a "dust atmosphere" that looks static but is composed of dust particles in constant motion. According to models proposed starting from 1956,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-townsendbrown_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-townsendbrown-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       on the daylit side of the Moon, solar
       <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">
        ultraviolet
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">
        X-ray
       </a>
       radiation is energetic enough to knock
       <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">
        electrons
       </a>
       out of atoms and molecules in the lunar soil. Positive charges build up until the tiniest particles of lunar dust (measuring 1 micrometre and smaller) are repelled from the surface and lofted anywhere from metres to kilometres high, with the smallest particles reaching the highest altitudes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-townsendbrown_16-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-townsendbrown-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moonfountains_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-moonfountains-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref002_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ref002-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref001_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ref001-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Eventually they fall back toward the surface where the process is repeated. On the night side, the dust is negatively charged by electrons in the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
        solar wind
       </a>
       . Indeed, the "fountain model" suggests that the night side would charge up to higher voltages than the day side, possibly launching dust particles to higher velocities and altitudes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moonfountains_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-moonfountains-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This effect could be further enhanced during the portion of the Moon's orbit where it passes through Earth's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetotail" title="Magnetotail">
        magnetotail
       </a>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tail_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tail-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       see
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field_of_the_Moon" title="Magnetic field of the Moon">
        Magnetic field of the Moon
       </a>
       for more detail. On the terminator there could be significant horizontal electric fields forming between the day and night areas, resulting in horizontal dust transport.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tail_20-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-tail-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Also, the Moon has been shown to have a "
       <a href="/wiki/Sodium_tail_of_the_Moon" title="Sodium tail of the Moon">
        sodium tail
       </a>
       " too faint to be detected by the human eye. It is hundreds of thousands of miles long, and was discovered in 1998 as a result of
       <a href="/wiki/Boston_University" title="Boston University">
        Boston University
       </a>
       scientists observing the
       <a href="/wiki/Leonids" title="Leonids">
        Leonid
       </a>
       meteor storm. The Moon is constantly releasing atomic sodium gas from its surface, and
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        solar
       </a>
       radiation pressure accelerates the sodium atoms in the anti-sunward direction, forming an elongated tail which points away from the Sun.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc-363105_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bbc-363105-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref003_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ref003-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-26oct_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa-26oct-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of April 2013, it had not yet been determined whether ionized sodium gas atoms or charged dust are the cause of the reported Moon glows.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sodium_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sodium-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_lander">
        Chinese lander
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Chinese lander">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       China's
       <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_3" title="Chang'e 3">
        Chang'e 3
       </a>
       spacecraft, which was launched on December 1, 2013, and entered lunar orbit on December 6,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-busistandard20131206_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-busistandard20131206-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       was expected to contaminate the tenuous lunar
       <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">
        exosphere
       </a>
       with both propellant from engine firings and lunar dust from the vehicle's landing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space20131121_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-space20131121-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While concern was expressed that this could disrupt LADEE's mission,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space20131121_26-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-space20131121-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       such as its baseline readings of the Moon's exosphere, it may instead provide additional science value since both the quantity and composition of the spacecraft's propulsion system exhaust are known.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-airspace20131030_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-airspace20131030-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Data from LADEE may be used to track the distribution and eventual dissipation of the exhaust and dust in the Moon's exosphere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-airspace20131030_27-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-airspace20131030-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space-trouble_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-space-trouble-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It may also be possible to observe the
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_water#Transport" title="Lunar water">
        migration of water
       </a>
       , one component of the exhaust, giving insight on how it is transported and becomes trapped around the lunar poles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sandt20131002_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sandt20131002-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_objectives">
        Mission objectives
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Mission objectives">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The LADEE mission was designed to address three major science goals:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spaceref-27450_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-spaceref-27450-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Determine the global density, composition, and time variability of the tenuous lunar
        <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">
         exosphere
        </a>
        before it is perturbed by further human activity;
       </li>
       <li>
        Determine if the Apollo astronaut sightings of diffuse emission at tens of kilometers above the surface were
        <a href="/wiki/Sodium_tail_of_the_Moon" title="Sodium tail of the Moon">
         sodium glow
        </a>
        or dust;
       </li>
       <li>
        Document the dust impactor environment (size, frequency) to help guide design engineering for the outpost and also future robotic missions;
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       and one
       <a href="/wiki/Technology_demonstration" title="Technology demonstration">
        technology demonstration
       </a>
       goal:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Demonstrate two-way
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laser_communication" title="Laser communication">
         laser communication
        </a>
        from lunar orbit.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-llcd_31-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-llcd-31">
          [31]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spaceflight_operations">
        Spaceflight operations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Spaceflight operations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Minotaur_V_-_LADEE_launch.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4169" data-file-width="3363" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Minotaur_V_-_LADEE_launch.jpg/170px-Minotaur_V_-_LADEE_launch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Minotaur_V_-_LADEE_launch.jpg/255px-Minotaur_V_-_LADEE_launch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Minotaur_V_-_LADEE_launch.jpg/340px-Minotaur_V_-_LADEE_launch.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Minotaur_V_-_LADEE_launch.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Lunar Atmosphere and Dust Environment Explorer during liftoff
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Launch_of_LADEE_aboard_Minotaur_V,_seen_from_Vienna,_Virginia.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1365" data-file-width="2048" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Launch_of_LADEE_aboard_Minotaur_V%2C_seen_from_Vienna%2C_Virginia.jpg/220px-Launch_of_LADEE_aboard_Minotaur_V%2C_seen_from_Vienna%2C_Virginia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Launch_of_LADEE_aboard_Minotaur_V%2C_seen_from_Vienna%2C_Virginia.jpg/330px-Launch_of_LADEE_aboard_Minotaur_V%2C_seen_from_Vienna%2C_Virginia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Launch_of_LADEE_aboard_Minotaur_V%2C_seen_from_Vienna%2C_Virginia.jpg/440px-Launch_of_LADEE_aboard_Minotaur_V%2C_seen_from_Vienna%2C_Virginia.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Launch_of_LADEE_aboard_Minotaur_V,_seen_from_Vienna,_Virginia.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         LADEE heads into orbit, as seen from Virginia (long-exposure photo)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Launch">
        Launch
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Launch">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       LADEE was launched on September 7, 2013, at 03:27 UTC (September 6, 11:27 p.m. EDT), from the
       <a href="/wiki/Wallops_Flight_Facility" title="Wallops Flight Facility">
        Wallops Flight Facility
       </a>
       at the
       <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_Regional_Spaceport" title="Mid-Atlantic Regional Spaceport">
        Mid-Atlantic Regional Spaceport
       </a>
       on a
       <a href="/wiki/Minotaur_V" title="Minotaur V">
        Minotaur V
       </a>
       carrier rocket.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-propsow_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-propsow-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This was the first lunar mission to be launched from that facility. The launch had the potential for visibility along much of the U.S. eastern seaboard, from Maine to South Carolina; clear weather allowed numerous observers from New York City to Virginia to observe the ascent, first stage cutoff and second stage ignition.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbc_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nbc-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As the Minotaur V is a
       <a href="/wiki/Solid-propellant_rocket" title="Solid-propellant rocket">
        solid-propellant rocket
       </a>
       , spacecraft
       <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">
        attitude control
       </a>
       on this mission operated a bit differently from a typical liquid-fueled rocket with more continuous
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Closed-loop_controller" title="Closed-loop controller">
        closed-loop feedback
       </a>
       . The first three Minotaur stages "fly a pre-programmed attitude profile" to gain velocity and deliver the vehicle to its preliminary trajectory, while the fourth stage is used to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3-axis_stabilized_spacecraft" title="3-axis stabilized spacecraft">
        modify
       </a>
       the flight profile and deliver the LADEE spacecraft into
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perigee" title="Perigee">
        perigee
       </a>
       for the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin-stabilized_satellite" title="Spin-stabilized satellite">
        spin-stabilized
       </a>
       fifth stage to then put the spacecraft into a
       <a href="/wiki/Elliptic_orbit" title="Elliptic orbit">
        highly elliptical
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_orbit" title="Geocentric orbit">
        orbit around Earth
       </a>
       —the first of three—to begin a month-long Lunar transit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sf1-20130921_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sf1-20130921-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       While now separated from the LADEE spacecraft, both the fourth and fifth stages of the Minotaur V reached orbit, and are now
       <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">
        space debris
       </a>
       in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_orbit" title="Earth orbit">
        Earth orbit
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf20130906_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-nsf20130906-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_transit">
        Lunar transit
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Lunar transit">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       LADEE took an unusual approach in its transit of the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       . Launched into a
       <a href="/wiki/Elliptic_orbit" title="Elliptic orbit">
        highly elliptical
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_orbit" title="Earth orbit">
        Earth orbit
       </a>
       , the spacecraft made three increasingly larger laps around Earth
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf20130906_3-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-nsf20130906-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       before getting close enough to enter into
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selenocentric_orbit" title="Selenocentric orbit">
        Lunar orbit
       </a>
       . The transit required approximately one month.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-st20130907_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-st20130907-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LADEE_fires_small_engines.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="7056" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/LADEE_fires_small_engines.jpg/220px-LADEE_fires_small_engines.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/LADEE_fires_small_engines.jpg/330px-LADEE_fires_small_engines.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/LADEE_fires_small_engines.jpg/440px-LADEE_fires_small_engines.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LADEE_fires_small_engines.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Artist concept of LADEE firing thrusters
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       After separating from the Minotaur, high electrical currents were detected in the satellite's
       <a href="/wiki/Reaction_wheel" title="Reaction wheel">
        reaction wheels
       </a>
       causing them to be shut down. There was no indication of a fault, and after the protection limits were adjusted, orientation with reaction wheels was resumed the following day.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfn-20130907_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sfn-20130907-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LROviewsLADEEorbitingMOON-20140114.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/LROviewsLADEEorbitingMOON-20140114.jpg/220px-LROviewsLADEEorbitingMOON-20140114.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/LROviewsLADEEorbitingMOON-20140114.jpg/330px-LROviewsLADEEorbitingMOON-20140114.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/LROviewsLADEEorbitingMOON-20140114.jpg/440px-LROviewsLADEEorbitingMOON-20140114.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LROviewsLADEEorbitingMOON-20140114.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter">
          LRO
         </a>
         views
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LADEE" title="LADEE">
          LADEE
         </a>
         about 9 km (5.6 mi) away orbiting the moon (January 14, 2014).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The LADEE spacecraft made three "phasing
       <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_orbit" title="Geocentric orbit">
        orbits
       </a>
       " of Earth before it accomplished a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit_insertion" title="Lunar orbit insertion">
        Lunar orbit insertion
       </a>
       (LOI), which occurred at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perigee" title="Perigee">
        perigee
       </a>
       of the third orbit using a three-minute engine burn.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf20130906_3-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-nsf20130906-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The target orbit for the third Earth orbit had a perigee of 200 kilometers (120 mi), an apogee of 278,000 km (173,000 mi) and an
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">
        inclination
       </a>
       of 37.65 degrees. The planned
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argument_of_perigee" title="Argument of perigee">
        argument of perigee
       </a>
       is 155 degrees, while its
       <a href="/wiki/Characteristic_energy" title="Characteristic energy">
        characteristic energy
       </a>
       , C3 is -2.75 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       /s
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf20130906_3-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-nsf20130906-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The novel trajectory using
       <a href="/wiki/Orbit_phasing" title="Orbit phasing">
        orbital phasing loops
       </a>
       was done for four main reasons:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ag20130912_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ag20130912-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The Minotaur V launch vehicle had insufficient
        <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">
         delta-v
        </a>
        to put the 383 kg (844 lb) LADEE directly into a
        <a href="/wiki/Trans-lunar_injection" title="Trans-lunar injection">
         trans-lunar injection
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        To handle potential off-nominal launch dispersions from the Minotaur V—which is a stack of five
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid-fuel_rocket" title="Solid-fuel rocket">
         solid rocket
        </a>
        stages, and is not considered to be a particularly precise rocket—in a propellant-efficient manner while leaving the orbit profile flexible to large dispersions in the initial injection orbit.
       </li>
       <li>
        To widen the
        <a href="/wiki/Launch_window" title="Launch window">
         launch window
        </a>
        to five days. In the event, LADEE did not need this as the launch occurred at the beginning of the window on the first day.
       </li>
       <li>
        To increase mission robustness in the face of any anomalous or missed orbital maneuvers with the spacecraft.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_orbit_and_systems_checkout">
        Lunar orbit and systems checkout
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Lunar orbit and systems checkout">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       LADEE entered lunar orbit on October 6, 2013, when LADEE was put into an elliptical capture orbit of 24 hours duration.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ag20131007_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ag20131007-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       LADEE was further lowered into a four-hour orbit on October 9, 2013,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ag20131009_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ag20131009-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One further burn occurred on October 12 lowering LADEE into a
       <a href="/wiki/Circular_orbit" title="Circular orbit">
        circular
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit" title="Lunar orbit">
        orbit around the Moon
       </a>
       with an altitude of approximately 250 kilometers (160 mi) for its commissioning phase, which lasted about 30 days.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdc20131007_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sdc20131007-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       LADEE's systems and instruments were checked out after the orbit was lowered to 75 km (47 mi) altitude.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf20130906_3-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-nsf20130906-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_Laser_Communication_Demonstration">
        Lunar Laser Communication Demonstration
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Lunar Laser Communication Demonstration">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Laser_communication_in_space" title="Laser communication in space">
        Laser communication in space
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LADEE%27s_LLCD_optical_module_rendering.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="754" data-file-width="1000" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/LADEE%27s_LLCD_optical_module_rendering.jpg/220px-LADEE%27s_LLCD_optical_module_rendering.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/LADEE%27s_LLCD_optical_module_rendering.jpg/330px-LADEE%27s_LLCD_optical_module_rendering.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/LADEE%27s_LLCD_optical_module_rendering.jpg/440px-LADEE%27s_LLCD_optical_module_rendering.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LADEE%27s_LLCD_optical_module_rendering.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Depiction of the optical module of the LLCD
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       LADEE's
       <i>
        Lunar Laser Communication Demonstration
       </i>
       (LLCD)
       <a href="/wiki/Pulsed_laser" title="Pulsed laser">
        pulsed laser
       </a>
       system conducted a successful test on October 18, 2013, transmitting data between the spacecraft and its ground station on Earth at a distance of 385,000 kilometres (239,000 mi). This test set a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downlink" title="Downlink">
        downlink
       </a>
       record of 622
       <a href="/wiki/Megabit" title="Megabit">
        megabits
       </a>
       per second (Mbps) from spacecraft to ground, and an "error-free data upload rate of 20 Mbps" from ground station to spacecraft.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parabolic_20131022_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-parabolic_20131022-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Tests were carried out over a 30-day test period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The LLCD is a
       <a href="/wiki/Free-space_optical_communication" title="Free-space optical communication">
        free-space optical communication
       </a>
       system. It is NASA's first attempt at two-way space communication using an
       <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">
        optical laser
       </a>
       instead of
       <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">
        radio waves
       </a>
       . It is expected to lead to operational laser systems on future NASA satellites. The next iteration of the concept will be the
       <a href="/wiki/Laser_Communications_Relay_Demonstration" title="Laser Communications Relay Demonstration">
        Laser Communications Relay Demonstration
       </a>
       scheduled for 2017.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parabolic_20131022_41-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-parabolic_20131022-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Also, it has been proposed as payload for the
       <a href="/wiki/Phobos_And_Deimos_%26_Mars_Environment" title="Phobos And Deimos &amp; Mars Environment">
        Phobos And Deimos &amp; Mars Environment
       </a>
       (PADME) orbiter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reyes_2014_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Reyes_2014-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Science_phase">
        Science phase
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Science phase">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       For the science operations, LADEE was maneuvered into an orbit with a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periselene" title="Periselene">
        periselene
       </a>
       of 20 km (12 mi) and an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aposelene" title="Aposelene">
        aposelene
       </a>
       of 60 km (37 mi).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQ_1-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-FAQ-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The science phase of LADEE's primary mission was initially planned as 100 days,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf20130906_3-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-nsf20130906-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and later given a 28-day extension. The extension provided an opportunity for the satellite to gather an additional full lunar cycle worth of very low-altitude data to help scientists unravel the nature of the Moon's tenuous exosphere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extension_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-extension-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_mission">
        End of mission
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: End of mission">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Spacecraft controllers ordered a final engine burn on April 11, 2014, to lower LADEE to within 2 km (1 mi) of the Moon's surface and set it up for impact no later than April 21.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20140418_8-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-nyt20140418-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc20140418_9-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-abc20140418-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20140418_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa20140418-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The probe then dealt with the
       <a href="/wiki/April_2014_lunar_eclipse" title="April 2014 lunar eclipse">
        April 2014 lunar eclipse
       </a>
       on April 15, during which it could not generate power because it was in Earth's shadow for four hours.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voanews20140418_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-voanews20140418-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Science instruments were turned off and heaters were cycled during the event to conserve energy but keep the spacecraft warm.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voanews20140418_46-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-voanews20140418-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Engineers did not expect LADEE to survive, as it was not designed to handle such an environment, but it exited the eclipse with only malfunctions in some pressure sensors.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc20140418_9-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-abc20140418-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During its penultimate orbit on April 17, LADEE's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periapsis" title="Periapsis">
        periapsis
       </a>
       took it within 300 m (1,000 ft) of the lunar surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csmonitor20140418_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-csmonitor20140418-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Contact with the spacecraft was lost around 04:30 UTC on April 18 when it moved behind the Moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20140418_8-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-nyt20140418-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-award_2014_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-award_2014-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       LADEE struck the Moon's far side surface some time between 04:30 and 05:22 at a speed of 5,800 km/h (3,600 mph); scientists will attempt to narrow down the impact time and location.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc20140418_9-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-abc20140418-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csmonitor20140418_47-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-csmonitor20140418-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers">
          <span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (October 2015)">
           needs update
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The far side of the Moon was chosen to avoid the possibility of damaging historically important locations such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Luna_programme" title="Luna programme">
        Luna
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">
        Apollo
       </a>
       landing sites.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20140418_8-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-nyt20140418-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       NASA used the
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter">
        Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter
       </a>
       to image the impact location.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141028-NNJ_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141028-NNJ-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20140418_45-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa20140418-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csmonitor20140418_47-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-csmonitor20140418-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tnone center">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:591px;max-width:591px">
        <div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">

          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft">
        Spacecraft
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Spacecraft">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Design">
        Design
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Design">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       LADEE is the first spacecraft
       <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_design" title="Spacecraft design">
        designed
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Integration_testing" title="Integration testing">
        integrated
       </a>
       , built, and
       <a href="/wiki/System_testing" title="System testing">
        tested
       </a>
       by NASA's
       <a href="/wiki/Ames_Research_Center" title="Ames Research Center">
        Ames Research Center
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdc20130909_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sdc20130909-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The spacecraft is of a novel design (a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spacecraft_bus" title="Spacecraft bus">
        spacecraft bus
       </a>
       never previously flown)—and of much lower cost than typical NASA science missions—which presented novel challenges to the trajectory design team in getting the new spacecraft launched to the Moon with a high-confidence spaceflight trajectory plan, while dealing with a first-use new rocket (Minotaur V) and a spacecraft with no
       <a href="/wiki/Flight_test" title="Flight test">
        flight test
       </a>
       legacy. (see
       <a href="#Lunar_transit">
        Lunar transit
       </a>
       , above.)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdc20130909_49-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-sdc20130909-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       LADEE mission makes use of the
       <a href="/wiki/Modular_Common_Spacecraft_Bus" title="Modular Common Spacecraft Bus">
        Modular Common Spacecraft Bus
       </a>
       , or body, made of a lightweight carbon composite with an unfueled mass of 248.2 kg (547 lb). The bus has the ability to perform on various kinds of missions—including voyages to the Moon and
       <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_object" title="Near-Earth object">
        Near-Earth objects
       </a>
       —with different modules or applicable systems. This modular concept is an innovative way of transitioning away from custom designs and toward multi-use designs and assembly-line production, which could dramatically reduce the cost of spacecraft development.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-missionpages_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa-missionpages-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The LADEE spacecraft bus modules consist of the Radiator Module which carries the avionics, electrical system, and attitude sensors; the Bus Module; the Payload Module that carries the two largest instruments; and the Extension Modules, which house the propulsion system.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQ_1-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-FAQ-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Specifications
       </dt>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The main structure is 2.37 m (7.8 ft) high, 1.85 m (6.1 ft) wide and 1.85 m (6.1 ft) deep. The total mass of the spacecraft is 383 kg (844 lb).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQ_1-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-FAQ-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Power">
        Power
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Power">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Electrical power is generated by a
       <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_system" title="Photovoltaic system">
        photovoltaic system
       </a>
       composed of 30 panels of silicon solar cells producing 295
       <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">
        W
       </a>
       at one
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">
        AU
       </a>
       . The solar panels are mounted on the satellite's exterior surfaces and the electrical power is stored in one
       <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery" title="Lithium-ion battery">
        lithium-ion battery
       </a>
       providing up to 24
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ampere-hour" title="Ampere-hour">
        amp-hours
       </a>
       of 28-
       <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">
        volt
       </a>
       power.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQ_1-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-FAQ-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Propulsion_system">
        Propulsion system
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Propulsion system">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The LADEE propulsion system consisted of an orbit control system (OCS) and a reaction control system (RCS). The OCS provided velocity control along the +
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Z_axis" title="Z axis">
        Z axis
       </a>
       for large velocity adjustments. The RCS provided three-axis
       <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">
        attitude control
       </a>
       during burns of the OCS system, and also provided momentum dumps for the
       <a href="/wiki/Reaction_wheel" title="Reaction wheel">
        reaction wheels
       </a>
       which were the primary attitude control system between OCS burns.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-propsow_32-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-propsow-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The main engine was a 455
       <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">
        N
       </a>
       High Performance Apogee Thruster (HiPAT). The high efficiency 22N attitude control thrusters are manufactured using high temperature materials and similar to the HiPAT. The main engine provided the majority of the thrust for spacecraft trajectory correction maneuvers. The control system thrusters were used for the small maneuvers planned for the science phase of the mission.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQ_1-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-FAQ-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Following the science phase, a decommissioning period occurred, during which the altitude was gradually lowered until the spacecraft impacted the lunar surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAQ_1-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-FAQ-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Science_payload">
        Science payload
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Science payload">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       LADEE carried three scientific instruments and a technology demonstration payload.
      </p>
      <p>
       The science payload consists of:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The Neutral Mass Spectrometer (NMS), which performed in situ measurements of
        <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">
         exospheric
        </a>
        atoms and molecules via
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_spectroscopy" title="Mass spectroscopy">
         mass spectroscopy
        </a>
        . Parts of NMS were based on the SAM instrument on the
        <a href="/wiki/Mars_Science_Laboratory" title="Mars Science Laboratory">
         Mars Science Laboratory
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The UV-Vis Spectrometer (UVS), which measured both the dust and exosphere by
        <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet%E2%80%93visible_spectroscopy" title="Ultraviolet–visible spectroscopy">
         ultraviolet–visible spectroscopy
        </a>
        . The instrument was based on the UV-Vis spectrometer on the
        <a href="/wiki/LCROSS" title="LCROSS">
         LCROSS
        </a>
        mission.
       </li>
       <li>
        Lunar Dust EXperiment (LDEX), which directly measured dust using an impact ionization detector. This functions by measuring the ionization of particles hitting the detector.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
         <a href="#cite_note-52">
          [52]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The instrument built on experience gained from similar instruments on
        <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
         Galileo
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_(spacecraft)" title="Ulysses (spacecraft)">
         Ulysses
        </a>
        and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cassini_(spacecraft)" title="Cassini (spacecraft)">
         Cassini
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       LADEE also carried a technology demonstration payload for testing an
       <a href="/wiki/Optical_communication" title="Optical communication">
        optical communication
       </a>
       system. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Laser_Communication_Demonstration" title="Lunar Laser Communication Demonstration">
        Lunar Laser Communication Demonstration
       </a>
       (LLCD) used a laser to transmit and receive data as pulses of light, in much the same way as data is transferred in a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fiber_optic" title="Fiber optic">
        fiber optic
       </a>
       cable. Three ground stations were used. This method of communication could potentially provide data rates five times higher than the previous
       <a href="/wiki/Radio_frequency" title="Radio frequency">
        radio frequency
       </a>
       communication system.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-llcd_31-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-llcd-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gsfc-335_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-gsfc-335-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The technology is a direct predecessor to NASA's
       <a href="/wiki/Laser_Communications_Relay_Demonstration" title="Laser Communications Relay Demonstration">
        Laser Communications Relay Demonstration
       </a>
       (LCRD) system which is due to launch in 2017.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa-laser_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-esa-laser-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-lcd_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa-lcd-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed center">
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 140px">
        <div style="width: 140px">
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 138px;">
          <div style="margin:0px auto;">

           </a>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallerytext">
          <p>
           LADEE with instruments labeled
          </p>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 177.33333333333px">
        <div style="width: 177.33333333333px">
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 175.33333333333px;">
          <div style="margin:0px auto;">

           </a>
          </div>
         </div>

          </p>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 142px">

           </a>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallerytext">
          <p>
           UVS
          </p>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 180.66666666667px">
        <div style="width: 180.66666666667px">
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 178.66666666667px;">
          <div style="margin:0px auto;">

          </p>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Preliminary_results">
        Preliminary results
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Preliminary results">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The LADEE science teams continued to analyze data acquired at the time of the Chang'e 3 landing on December 14, 2013.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-results_Jan_2014_56-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-results_Jan_2014-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The Lunar Dust EXperiment (LDEX) team noted an increase in dust around the time of the landing. However, the rise preceded the landing time by many hours, suggesting a different origin. Indeed, the
        <a href="/wiki/Geminids" title="Geminids">
         Geminids
        </a>
        meteor shower coincided with this landing event and produced elevated dust counts before, during and after the landing period.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-results_Jan_2014_56-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-results_Jan_2014-56">
          [56]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The team reported that "if LADEE did encounter any lunar soil particles thrown up by the final descent of Chang'e 3, they would have been lost in the background of Geminid-produced events."
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-results_Jan_2014_56-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-results_Jan_2014-56">
          [56]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The Neutral Mass Spectrometer (NMS) team has been searching the data for exhaust gas species such as water, carbon monoxide and carbon dioxide (CO and CO
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
        ) as well as nitrogen (N
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
        ).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-results_Jan_2014_56-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-results_Jan_2014-56">
          [56]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The Ultraviolet and Visible light Spectrometer (UVS) carried out a series of before/after observations looking for effects of both the landing and meteor showers. Analysis revealed an increase in sodium in the exosphere in connection with the Geminid meteor shower, as well as evidence of increased light scattering due to dust. The UVS also has been monitoring emission lines of atomic oxygen, and has seen emissions that may indicate the presence of both iron (Fe) and titanium (
        <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">
         Ti
        </a>
        ), which were expected but they have never before been observed.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-results_Jan_2014_56-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-results_Jan_2014-56">
          [56]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
         Helium
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">
         argon
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">
         neon
        </a>
        gases were determined to be the most abundant species in the lunar exosphere.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-update_57-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-update-57">
          [57]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Texas_2014_58-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Texas_2014-58">
          [58]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The helium and neon were found to be supplied by the
        <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
         solar wind
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-update_57-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-update-57">
          [57]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        On 17 August 2015, based on studies with the LADEE spacecraft, NASA scientists reported the detection of
        <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">
         neon
        </a>
        in the
        <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">
         exosphere
        </a>
        of the
        <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
         moon
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150817_59-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150817-59">
          [59]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Team">
        Team
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Team">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The team for LADEE includes contributors from NASA Headquarters, Washington D.C., NASA's Ames Research Center, Moffett Field, California, NASA's Goddard Space Flight Center,
       <a href="/wiki/Greenbelt,_Maryland" title="Greenbelt, Maryland">
        Greenbelt, Maryland
       </a>
       , and the Laboratory for Atmospheric and Space Physics at the University of Colorado at Boulder.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-team_60-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-team-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Guest investigators include those from the University of California, Berkeley;The Johns Hopkins University Applied Physics Laboratory, Laurel, Maryland; the University of Colorado; the University of Maryland; and NASA's Goddard Space Flight Center, Greenbelt, Maryland.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-team_60-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-team-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
